<p><b>Sir Nicholas William Peter Clegg</b> (born 7 January 1967) is a British politician who served as <a href="/wiki/Deputy_Prime_Minister_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Deputy Prime Minister of the United Kingdom">Deputy Prime Minister of the United Kingdom</a> from 2010 to 2015 and as <a href="/wiki/Leader_of_the_Liberal_Democrats" title="Leader of the Liberal Democrats">Leader of the Liberal Democrats</a> from 2007 to 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-2">[2]</a></sup> An "<a href="/wiki/The_Orange_Book:_Reclaiming_Liberalism" title="The Orange Book: Reclaiming Liberalism">Orange Book</a>" liberal,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-orangebook_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-orangebook-3">[3]</a></sup> Clegg served as a Member of Parliament (MP) for <a href="/wiki/Sheffield_Hallam_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Sheffield Hallam (UK Parliament constituency)">Sheffield Hallam</a> from 2005 to 2017 and has been associated with both <a href="/wiki/Social_liberalism" title="Social liberalism">socially liberal</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Economically_liberal" title="Economically liberal">economically liberal</a> policies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>Born in <a href="/wiki/Buckinghamshire" title="Buckinghamshire">Buckinghamshire</a>, Clegg was educated at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Cambridge" title="University of Cambridge">University of Cambridge</a>, the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Minnesota" title="University of Minnesota">University of Minnesota</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/College_of_Europe" title="College of Europe">College of Europe</a>. He served as a journalist for the <a href="/wiki/Financial_Times" title="Financial Times">Financial Times</a> before becoming a <a href="/wiki/Member_of_the_European_Parliament" title="Member of the European Parliament">Member of the European Parliament</a> (MEP) in 1999.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> After his election to the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons" title="House of Commons">House of Commons</a> in 2005, Clegg served in a variety of leadership roles in the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Democrats_(UK)" title="Liberal Democrats (UK)">Liberal Democrats</a>, most notably as Spokesperson for Home Affairs, before being elected to succeed <a href="/wiki/Menzies_Campbell" title="Menzies Campbell">Menzies Campbell</a> as party leader in 2007. During his tenure as leader, Clegg asserted that the Liberal Democrats <a href="/wiki/Triangulation_(politics)" title="Triangulation (politics)">transcended</a> the tradition "left-right" axis and labelled the party as being <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radical_centrist" title="Radical centrist">radical centrist</a> in orientation. He advocated for reduced taxes, electoral reform, cuts on defence spending, and an increased focus on environmental issues.
</p><p>As a result of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2010_general_election_(UK)" title="2010 general election (UK)">2010 general election</a>, Clegg's Liberal Democrats found themselves with 57 seats in the House of Commons. The <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservative Party</a>, which failed to receive a majority, formed a coalition with the Liberal Democrats, and Clegg was appointed by David Cameron to serve as his Deputy Prime Minister. In this capacity, he became the first leader of the Liberal Democrats to answer for the Prime Minister's Questions, and used his influence in the position to pass the <a href="/wiki/Fixed-term_Parliaments_Act_2011" title="Fixed-term Parliaments Act 2011">Fixed-term Parliaments Act</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-takebackpower1_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-takebackpower1-7">[7]</a></sup> Controversy arose during this time surrounding the Liberal Democrats' decision to abandon to their pledge to oppose increases in tuition fees, which had previously been a key issue that won the party support from students.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-libdems6_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-libdems6-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the party's time in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coalition_Government_2010-2015" title="Coalition Government 2010-2015">coalition</a>, the Liberal Democrats saw a significant drop in support,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thisislondon1_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-thisislondon1-9">[9]</a></sup>  and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2015_election_(UK)" title="2015 election (UK)">2015 election</a> left the party with just 8 seats, which resulted in Clegg's ousting as Deputy Prime Minister and his resignation as party leader.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> In 2016, following a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brexit_referendum" title="Brexit referendum">referendum</a> in which a majority supported leaving the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>, Clegg returned to the Liberal Democrat <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frontbench" title="Frontbench">frontbench</a>, concurrently serving as Spokesperson for Exiting the European Union and for International Trade from July 2016 to June 2017. In the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_2017" title="United Kingdom general election, 2017">2017 general election</a>, Clegg was defeated in his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constituency_(UK)" title="Constituency (UK)">constituency</a> of Sheffield Hallam by <a href="/wiki/Jared_O%27Mara" title="Jared O'Mara">Jared O'Mara</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">Labour Party</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-telegraph2_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-telegraph2-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life_and_family">Early life and family</span></h2>
<p>Clegg was born in <a href="/wiki/Chalfont_St_Giles" title="Chalfont St Giles">Chalfont St Giles</a>, Buckinghamshire, the third of four children of Hermance van den Wall Bake and <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_P._Clegg" title="Nicholas P. Clegg">Nicholas Peter Clegg</a> <span style="font-size:85%;"><a href="/wiki/Order_of_the_British_Empire" title="Order of the British Empire">CBE</a></span>, chairman of United Trust Bank<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> and a former trustee of the <a href="/wiki/Daiwa_Anglo-Japanese_Foundation" title="Daiwa Anglo-Japanese Foundation">Daiwa Anglo-Japanese Foundation</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> (where <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_Clarke" title="Kenneth Clarke">Ken Clarke</a> was an adviser).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>Clegg is of one-quarter <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baltic-German" title="Baltic-German">Baltic-German</a> descent: his paternal grandmother, <a href="/wiki/Engelhardt_family" title="Engelhardt family">Kira von Engelhardt, Baroness von Smolensk</a>, was a Baltic-German noblewoman, niece of <a href="/wiki/Moura_Budberg" title="Moura Budberg">Moura Budberg</a>, adventuress and suspected <a href="/wiki/Double_agent" title="Double agent">double agent</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> and the granddaughter of <a href="/wiki/Attorney_general" title="Attorney general">attorney general</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Imperial_Russia" title="Imperial Russia">Imperial Russian</a> Senate, Ignatiy Platonovich Zakrevsky.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thepeerage_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-thepeerage-19">[19]</a></sup> Through this Russian connection, Clegg is distantly related to <a href="/wiki/Michael_Ignatieff" title="Michael Ignatieff">Michael Ignatieff</a>, leader of the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_of_Canada" title="Liberal Party of Canada">Liberal Party of Canada</a> from 2008 to 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>His English grandfather was <a href="/wiki/Hugh_Clegg_(doctor)" title="Hugh Clegg (doctor)">Hugh Anthony Clegg</a>, editor of the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BMJ" title="BMJ">British Medical Journal</a></i> for 35 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-telegraph1_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-telegraph1-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>Clegg's mother is Dutch<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup>  and was <a href="/wiki/Internment" title="Internment">interned</a>, along with her family, by the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Japanese_Army" title="Imperial Japanese Army">Japanese military</a> in <a href="/wiki/History_of_Jakarta" title="History of Jakarta">Batavia</a> (Jakarta) in the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_East_Indies" title="Dutch East Indies">Dutch East Indies</a> (Indonesia) during the <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">Second World War</a>. She met Clegg's father during a visit to England in 1956,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-telegraph1_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-telegraph1-21">[21]</a></sup> and they married on 1 August 1959.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>Clegg is <a href="/wiki/Multilingualism" title="Multilingualism">multilingual</a>: he speaks English, French, Dutch, German, and Spanish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lang1_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-lang1-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lang2_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-lang2-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lang3_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-lang3-26">[26]</a></sup> His background has informed his politics. He says, "There is simply not a shred of racism in me, as a person whose whole family is formed by flight from persecution, from different people in different generations. It's what I am. It's one of the reasons I am a liberal."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> His Dutch mother instilled in him "a degree of scepticism about the entrenched <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Social_structure_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Social structure of the United Kingdom">class configurations in British society</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ekklesia_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-ekklesia-28">[28]</a></sup> He has said of languages that "The danger is that we [in the UK] can afford to be lazy about languages, because they all want to speak English – English is the most useful, the global language bar none. But I don't think we should allow that luxury to be a sort of alibi not to learn languages."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Westminster_School_Arch.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Westminster_School_Arch.jpg/220px-Westminster_School_Arch.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Westminster_School_Arch.jpg/330px-Westminster_School_Arch.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Westminster_School_Arch.jpg/440px-Westminster_School_Arch.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Westminster_School_Arch.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Westminster School</div></div></div>
<p>Clegg was educated at two <a href="/wiki/Independent_school_(United_Kingdom)" title="Independent school (United Kingdom)">independent schools</a>: at <a href="/wiki/Caldicott_School" title="Caldicott School">Caldicott School</a> in <a href="/wiki/Farnham_Royal" title="Farnham Royal">Farnham Royal</a> in South <a href="/wiki/Buckinghamshire" title="Buckinghamshire">Buckinghamshire</a>, where he was joint Head Prefect in 1980,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-onlinenigeria_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-onlinenigeria-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> and later at <a href="/wiki/Westminster_School" title="Westminster School">Westminster School</a> in <a href="/wiki/Central_London" title="Central London">Central London</a>. As a 16-year-old <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exchange_student" title="Exchange student">exchange student</a> in Munich, he and a friend drunkenly set fire to what he called "the leading collection of <a href="/wiki/Cactus" title="Cactus">cacti</a> in Germany". When news of the incident was reported during his time as Liberal Democrat <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Home_affairs" title="Home affairs">home affairs</a> spokesman, Clegg said he was "not proud" of it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> He was arrested and not formally charged, but performed a kind of <a href="/wiki/Community_service" title="Community service">community service</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>He spent a <a href="/wiki/Gap_year" title="Gap year">gap year</a> working as a skiing instructor in Austria, before going on to <a href="/wiki/University_of_Cambridge" title="University of Cambridge">Cambridge University</a> in 1986, where he studied Archaeology and Anthropology at <a href="/wiki/Robinson_College,_Cambridge" title="Robinson College, Cambridge">Robinson College</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bb_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-bb-35">[35]</a></sup> He was active in the student theatre at Cambridge, acting in a play about AIDS and under director <a href="/wiki/Sam_Mendes" title="Sam Mendes">Sam Mendes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bb_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-bb-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-typepad1_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-typepad1-37">[37]</a></sup> He was also captain of his college's tennis team, and campaigned for the human rights organisation <a href="/wiki/Survival_International" title="Survival International">Survival International</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> Clegg spent the summer of 1989 as an office junior in <a href="/wiki/Postipankki" title="Postipankki">Postipankki</a> bank in <a href="/wiki/Helsinki" title="Helsinki">Helsinki</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>It has been alleged that Clegg joined the <a href="/wiki/Cambridge_University_Conservative_Association" title="Cambridge University Conservative Association">Cambridge University Conservative Association</a> between 1986 and 1987. Clegg has maintained he has "no recollection of that whatsoever". However, Conservative MP <a href="/wiki/Greg_Hands" title="Greg Hands">Greg Hands</a> has a record of CUCA members for 1986–1987, and Clegg's name appears on the list. Hands noted that "for the avoidance of any doubt, there was only one 'N Clegg' at Robinson College  ... [he] is listed in the 'Robinson College Record', under 'Freshmen 1986'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-blogs_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-blogs-43">[43]</a></sup> He graduated with an upper second class honours (2:1) degree in social anthropology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lrb_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-lrb-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>After university, he was awarded a scholarship to study for a year at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Minnesota" title="University of Minnesota">University of Minnesota</a>, where he wrote a thesis on the political philosophy of the <a href="/wiki/Deep_ecology" title="Deep ecology">Deep Green movement</a>. He then moved to New York City, where he worked as an intern under <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Hitchens" title="Christopher Hitchens">Christopher Hitchens</a> at <i><a href="/wiki/The_Nation" title="The Nation">The Nation</a></i>, a <a href="/wiki/Liberalism" title="Liberalism">progressive liberal</a> magazine, where he fact-checked Hitchens's articles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC_Daily_Politics_profile_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_Daily_Politics_profile-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>Clegg next moved to <a href="/wiki/Brussels" title="Brussels">Brussels</a>, where he worked alongside <a href="/wiki/Guy_Spier" title="Guy Spier">Guy Spier</a> for six months as a trainee in the G24 co-ordination unit which delivered aid to the countries of the former <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>. After the internship he studied for a master's degree at the <a href="/wiki/College_of_Europe" title="College of Europe">College of Europe</a> in <a href="/wiki/Bruges" title="Bruges">Bruges</a>, a university for European studies in Belgium, where he met his wife, <a href="/wiki/Miriam_Gonz%C3%A1lez_Dur%C3%A1ntez" title="Miriam González Durántez">Miriam González Durántez</a>, a lawyer and the daughter of a Spanish senator.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-typepad1_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-typepad1-37">[37]</a></sup> Nick Clegg is an alumnus of the "<a href="/wiki/College_of_Europe#Promotions" title="College of Europe">Mozart Promotion</a>" (1991–92) of the College of Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Careers_outside_politics">Careers outside politics</span></h2>
<p>Between 1992 and 1993, he was employed by GJW Government Relations Ltd, which lobbied on behalf of <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">Libya</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1993, Clegg won the inaugural <i><a href="/wiki/Financial_Times" title="Financial Times">Financial Times</a></i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span> David Thomas Prize, in remembrance of an <i>FT</i> journalist killed on assignment in <a href="/wiki/Kuwait" title="Kuwait">Kuwait</a> in 1991. He was later sent to Hungary, where he wrote articles about the mass privatisation of industries in the former <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Bloc" title="Eastern Bloc">communist bloc</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-typepad1_37-2"><a href="#cite_note-typepad1-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p>He took up a post at the <a href="/wiki/European_Commission" title="European Commission">European Commission</a> in April 1994, working in the <a href="/wiki/Technical_Aid_to_the_Commonwealth_of_Independent_States" title="Technical Aid to the Commonwealth of Independent States">TACIS</a> aid programme to the <a href="/wiki/Post-Soviet_states" title="Post-Soviet states">former Soviet Union</a>. For two years, Clegg was responsible for developing direct aid programmes in Central Asia and the <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a> worth €50 million. He was involved in negotiations with Russia on airline overflight rights, and launched a conference in <a href="/wiki/Tashkent" title="Tashkent">Tashkent</a> in 1993 that founded <a href="/wiki/TRACECA" title="TRACECA">TRACECA</a>—an international transport programme for the development of a <a href="/wiki/Transport_corridor" title="Transport corridor">transport corridor</a> for Europe, the Caucasus and Asia. <a href="/wiki/Vice-President_of_the_European_Commission" title="Vice-President of the European Commission">Vice-President</a> and <a href="/wiki/European_Commissioner_for_Trade" title="European Commissioner for Trade">Trade Commissioner</a> <a href="/wiki/Leon_Brittan" title="Leon Brittan">Leon Brittan</a> then offered him a job in his private office, as a European Union policy adviser and speechwriter. As part of this role, Clegg was in charge of the EC negotiating team on Chinese and Russian accession talks to the <a href="/wiki/World_Trade_Organization" title="World Trade Organization">World Trade Organization</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-typepad1_37-3"><a href="#cite_note-typepad1-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Written_publications">Written publications</span></h3>
<p>Clegg has written extensively, publishing and contributing to a large number of pamphlets and books. With <a href="/wiki/Richard_Grayson_(academic)" title="Richard Grayson (academic)">Dr Richard Grayson</a> he wrote a book in 2002 about the importance of devolution in secondary education systems, based on comparative research across Europe. The final conclusions included the idea of <a href="/wiki/Pupil_premium" title="Pupil premium">pupil premiums</a> so that children from poorer backgrounds receive the additional resources their educational needs require.
</p><p>He wrote a controversial pamphlet for the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Centre_for_European_Reform&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Centre for European Reform (page does not exist)">Centre for European Reform</a> advocating devolution and evolution of the European Union, and contributed to the 2004 <i><a href="/wiki/The_Orange_Book:_Reclaiming_Liberalism" title="The Orange Book: Reclaiming Liberalism">Orange Book</a></i>, where he offered <a href="/wiki/Classical_liberalism" title="Classical liberalism">market liberal</a> solutions for reform of European institutions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-orangebook_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-orangebook-3">[3]</a></sup> He co-authored a pamphlet with Duncan Brack arguing for a wholesale reform of world trade rules to allow room for a greater emphasis on development, internationally binding environmental treaties, and <a href="/wiki/Parliamentary_system" title="Parliamentary system">parliamentary democracy</a> within the WTO system. In 2017, he wrote <i><a href="/wiki/How_To_Stop_Brexit_(And_Make_Britain_Great_Again)" title="How To Stop Brexit (And Make Britain Great Again)">How To Stop Brexit (And Make Britain Great Again)</a></i> which makes the case that <a href="/wiki/Brexit" title="Brexit">Brexit</a> is not inevitable.
</p>
<h2><span id="Member_of_the_European_Parliament_.281999.E2.80.932004.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Member_of_the_European_Parliament_(1999–2004)">Member of the European Parliament (1999–2004)</span></h2>
<p>In 1998, Clegg was selected as the lead Liberal Democrat candidate for the <a href="/wiki/European_Parliament" title="European Parliament">European Parliament</a> in the <a href="/wiki/East_Midlands_(European_Parliament_constituency)" title="East Midlands (European Parliament constituency)">East Midlands</a> constituency; the following year, <a href="/wiki/Paddy_Ashdown" title="Paddy Ashdown">Paddy Ashdown</a> was first to tip him as a politician to watch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cleggbackground_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-cleggbackground-50">[50]</a></sup> On his election in 1999, he was the first Liberal parliamentarian elected in the <a href="/wiki/East_Midlands" title="East Midlands">East Midlands</a> since <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ernest_Harold_Pickering" title="Ernest Harold Pickering">Ernest Pickering</a> was elected MP for <a href="/wiki/Leicester_West_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Leicester West (UK Parliament constituency)">Leicester West</a> in 1931, and was credited with helping to significantly boost the Liberal Democrat poll rating in the region in the six months after his election. Clegg worked extensively during his time as an MEP to support the party in the region, not least in <a href="/wiki/Chesterfield_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Chesterfield (UK Parliament constituency)">Chesterfield</a> where <a href="/wiki/Paul_Holmes_(politician)" title="Paul Holmes (politician)">Paul Holmes</a> was elected as MP in <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_2001" title="United Kingdom general election, 2001">2001</a>. Clegg helped persuade Conservative MEP <a href="/wiki/Bill_Newton_Dunn" title="Bill Newton Dunn">Bill Newton Dunn</a> to defect to the Liberal Democrats, with Newton Dunn subsequently succeeding him as MEP for the East Midlands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>As an MEP, Clegg co-founded the Campaign for Parliamentary Reform, which led calls for reforms to expenses, transparency and accountability in the European Parliament.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> He was made Trade and Industry spokesman for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_Liberal_Democrat_and_Reform_Party" title="European Liberal Democrat and Reform Party">European Liberal Democrat and Reform group</a> (ELDR).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> In December 2000, Nick Clegg became the Parliament's Draftsman on a complex new EU telecoms law relating to "<a href="/wiki/Local-loop_unbundling" title="Local-loop unbundling">local loop unbundling</a>"—opening-up telephone networks across Europe to competition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> Clegg decided to leave Brussels in 2002, arguing in an article in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Guardian" title="The Guardian">The Guardian</a></i> newspaper that the battle to persuade the public of the benefits of Europe was being fought at home, not in Brussels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2004, Clegg explained to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Select_Committee_(Westminster_system)" title="Select Committee (Westminster system)">Select Committee</a> on European Union that the aim of MEPs like himself, who had been active in the debate on the EU's negotiating mandate, was to obtain the right to ratify any major WTO deal entered into by the European Union.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> That same year he chaired a policy working group for the Liberal Democrats on the <a href="/wiki/Old_age#Third_age" title="Old age">Third Age</a>, which focused on the importance of ending the cliff-edge of retirement and providing greater opportunities for older people to remain active beyond retirement. The group developed initial proposals on transforming post offices to help them survive as community hubs, in particular for older people. He served on <a href="/wiki/Charles_Kennedy" title="Charles Kennedy">Charles Kennedy</a>'s policy review, "Meeting the Challenge", and the "It's About Freedom" working parties.
</p><p>Clegg, for four years whilst an MEP, wrote a fortnightly column for <i><a href="/wiki/TheGuardian.com" title="TheGuardian.com">Guardian Unlimited</a></i>. One particular article in 2002 accused <a href="/wiki/Gordon_Brown" title="Gordon Brown">Gordon Brown</a> of encouraging "condescension" towards Germany. In an article, Clegg wrote that "all nations have a cross to bear, and none more so than Germany with its memories of Nazism. But the British cross is more insidious still. A misplaced sense of superiority, sustained by delusions of grandeur and a tenacious obsession with the last war, is much harder to shake off".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> The article was dusted down during the 2010 general election campaign when the <i><a href="/wiki/Daily_Mail" title="Daily Mail">Daily Mail</a></i> interpreted the article as being a "Nazi slur on Britain" and Clegg had begun to feel the full heat of the British tabloid press following his success during the first <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election_debates,_2010" title="United Kingdom general election debates, 2010">leaders' debate</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Parliamentary_candidate">Parliamentary candidate</span></h2>
<p>On leaving the European Parliament, Clegg joined political lobbying firm GPlus in April 2004 as a fifth partner:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="cquote pullquote" role="presentation" style="margin:auto; border-collapse: collapse; border: none; background-color: transparent; width: auto;">
<tbody><tr>
<td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: top; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: 'Times New Roman', Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: left; padding: 10px 10px;">“
</td>
<td style="vertical-align: top; border: none; padding: 4px 10px;">It's especially exciting to be joining GPlus at a time when Brussels is moving more and more to the centre of business concerns. With the EU taking in ten more countries and adopting a new Constitution, organisations need more than ever intelligent professional help in engaging with the EU institutions.
</td>
<td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: bottom; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: 'Times New Roman', Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: right; padding: 10px 10px;">”
</td></tr>
</tbody></table>
<p>Clegg worked on GPlus clients including <a href="/wiki/The_Hertz_Corporation" title="The Hertz Corporation">The Hertz Corporation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Centrica" title="Centrica">British Gas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ch4FCApil2010_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ch4FCApil2010-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>In November 2004, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Richard_Allan" title="Richard Allan">Richard Allan</a>, the then Liberal Democrat MP for <a href="/wiki/Sheffield_Hallam_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Sheffield Hallam (UK Parliament constituency)">Sheffield Hallam</a>, announced his intention to stand down from the House of Commons. Clegg was selected as the prospective parliamentary candidate. He took up a part-time teaching position in the politics department of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Sheffield" title="University of Sheffield">University of Sheffield</a>, combining it with ongoing EU consultancy work with GPlus. He also gave a series of seminar lectures in the <a href="/wiki/International_relations" title="International relations">international relations</a> Department of the University of Cambridge.
</p>
<h2><span id="Member_of_Parliament_.282005.E2.80.932017.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Member_of_Parliament_(2005–2017)">Member of Parliament (2005–2017)</span></h2>
<p>Clegg worked closely with Allan throughout the campaign in Sheffield Hallam—including starring in a local pantomime—and won the seat in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_2005" title="United Kingdom general election, 2005">2005 general election</a> with over 50% of the vote, and a majority of 8,682.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> This result represents one of the smallest swings away from a party in a seat where an existing MP has been succeeded by a newcomer (4.3%). He also served as treasurer and secretary of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/All-Party_Parliamentary_Group" title="All-Party Parliamentary Group">All-Party Parliamentary Group on National Parks</a>, a particular interest given that his constituency includes part of the <a href="/wiki/Peak_District" title="Peak District">Peak District National Park</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>Following his election to parliament, Clegg was promoted by leader <a href="/wiki/Charles_Kennedy" title="Charles Kennedy">Charles Kennedy</a> to be the party's spokesperson on Europe, focusing on the party's preparations for an expected referendum on the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_establishing_a_Constitution_for_Europe" title="Treaty establishing a Constitution for Europe">European constitution</a> and acting as deputy to Foreign Affairs Spokesperson <a href="/wiki/Menzies_Campbell" title="Menzies Campbell">Menzies Campbell</a>. Clegg's ability to articulate liberal values at a very practical level quickly lent him prominence, with many<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (April 2017)">who?</span></a></i>]</sup> already seeing him as a future Liberal Democrat leader. Following the resignation of Kennedy on 7 January 2006, Clegg was touted as a possible leadership contender.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> He was quick to rule himself out however instead declaring his support for Menzies Campbell ahead of his former colleague in the European Parliament <a href="/wiki/Chris_Huhne" title="Chris Huhne">Chris Huhne</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> with Campbell going on to win the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Democrats_leadership_election,_2006" title="Liberal Democrats leadership election, 2006">ballot</a>. Clegg had been a signatory to the letter circulated by <a href="/wiki/Vince_Cable" title="Vince Cable">Vince Cable</a> prior to Kennedy's resignation, which stated his opposition to working under Kennedy's continued leadership.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Liberal_Democrats.27_Home_Affairs_spokesperson"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Liberal_Democrats'_Home_Affairs_spokesperson">Liberal Democrats' Home Affairs spokesperson</span></h3>
<p>After the 2006 leadership election, Clegg was promoted to be Home Affairs spokesperson, replacing <a href="/wiki/Mark_Oaten" title="Mark Oaten">Mark Oaten</a>. In this job he spearheaded the Liberal Democrats' defence of civil liberties, proposing a Freedom Bill to repeal what he described as "unnecessary and illiberal legislation",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> campaigning against <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_identity_card_(United_Kingdom)" title="National identity card (United Kingdom)">Identity Cards</a> and the retention of innocent people's DNA, and arguing against excessive counter-terrorism legislation. He has campaigned for prison reform, a liberal approach to immigration, and defended the <a href="/wiki/Human_Rights_Act_1998" title="Human Rights Act 1998">Human Rights Act</a> against ongoing attacks from across the political spectrum. In January 2007, Clegg launched the 'We Can Cut Crime!' campaign, "proposing real action at a national level and acting to cut crime where the Liberal Democrats are in power locally".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wecancutcrime_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-wecancutcrime-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Sir_Menzies_Campbell.27s_resignation"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Sir_Menzies_Campbell's_resignation">Sir Menzies Campbell's resignation</span></h3>
<p>Clegg caused a degree of controversy when, at the Liberal Democrat party conference in 2007, he admitted his leadership ambitions to journalists at a fringe event.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> The admission followed a period of increased media speculation about Sir Menzies Campbell's leadership, which the admission by Clegg did nothing to reduce and resulted in a rebuke by some of his frontbench colleagues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> This followed a report from <a href="/wiki/Kevin_Maguire_(journalist)" title="Kevin Maguire (journalist)">Kevin Maguire</a> in the <i><a href="/wiki/New_Statesman" title="New Statesman">New Statesman</a></i> that Clegg had failed to hide his disloyalty to Campbell's leadership.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> Campbell eventually resigned on 15 October 2007, saying that questions about his leadership were "getting in the way of further progress by the party".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nickcleggjesuischarlie.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2642" data-file-width="3496" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Nickcleggjesuischarlie.JPG/220px-Nickcleggjesuischarlie.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Nickcleggjesuischarlie.JPG/330px-Nickcleggjesuischarlie.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Nickcleggjesuischarlie.JPG/440px-Nickcleggjesuischarlie.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nickcleggjesuischarlie.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Nick Clegg attends the Je Suis Charlie rally with his wife Miriam González Durántez in Trafalgar Square, January 2015</div></div></div>
<h2><span id="Leader_of_the_Liberal_Democrats_.282007.E2.80.932015.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Leader_of_the_Liberal_Democrats_(2007–2015)">Leader of the Liberal Democrats (2007–2015)</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Election_to_the_leadership">Election to the leadership</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nickcleggcutv.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2623" data-file-width="4267" height="135" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Nickcleggcutv.png/220px-Nickcleggcutv.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Nickcleggcutv.png/330px-Nickcleggcutv.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Nickcleggcutv.png/440px-Nickcleggcutv.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nickcleggcutv.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Clegg at <a href="/wiki/Cardiff_University" title="Cardiff University">Cardiff University</a></div></div></div>
<p>After Campbell's resignation, Clegg was regarded by much of the media as front-runner in the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Democrats_leadership_election,_2007" title="Liberal Democrats leadership election, 2007">leadership election</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> The BBC's Political Editor <a href="/wiki/Nick_Robinson_(journalist)" title="Nick Robinson (journalist)">Nick Robinson</a> stated the election would be a two-horse race between Clegg and <a href="/wiki/Chris_Huhne" title="Chris Huhne">Chris Huhne</a> who had stood against Campbell in the 2006 election.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> On Friday 19 October 2007, Clegg launched his bid to become leader of the Liberal Democrats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> Clegg and Huhne clashed in the campaign over <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UK_Trident_programme" title="UK Trident programme">Trident</a> but were largely in agreement on many other issues. It was announced on 18 December that he had won.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> Clegg was appointed to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Her_Majesty%27s_Most_Honourable_Privy_Council" title="Her Majesty's Most Honourable Privy Council">Privy Council</a> (PC) on 30 January 2008, and affirmed his membership on 12 March 2008.
</p><p>In his acceptance speech upon winning the leadership contest, Clegg declared himself to be "a liberal by temperament, by instinct and by upbringing" and that he believes "Britain [is] a place of tolerance and <a href="/wiki/Cultural_pluralism" title="Cultural pluralism">pluralism</a>". He has stated that he feels "a profound antagonism for prejudice of all sorts".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ekklesia_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-ekklesia-28">[28]</a></sup> He declared his priorities as: defending civil liberties; devolving the running of public services to parents, pupils and patients; and protecting the environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p><p>In an interview on <a href="/wiki/BBC_Radio_5_Live" title="BBC Radio 5 Live">BBC Radio 5 Live</a> on the morning after his election to the leadership, Clegg stated that he does not believe in God, but that he has "an immense amount of respect for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/People_of_faith" title="People of faith">people of faith</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC-Clegg-God_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-Clegg-God-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-transcript_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-transcript-81">[81]</a></sup> In 2010, Clegg elaborated on this question, stating: "I was asked a question once in one of those questions where you're only allowed to answer "yes" or "no", and I was asked "Do you believe in God?" As it happens I don't know whether God exists. I'm much more of an agnostic."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-transcript_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-transcript-81">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p>He resigned as the leader of the Liberal Democrats after the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_2015" title="United Kingdom general election, 2015">2015 general election</a>. He said the results were "immeasurably more crushing and unkind than he feared".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> He was succeeded by <a href="/wiki/Tim_Farron" title="Tim Farron">Tim Farron</a> after a <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Democrats_leadership_election,_2015" title="Liberal Democrats leadership election, 2015">leadership election</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="GQ_magazine_interview_controversy"><i>GQ</i> magazine interview controversy</span></h4>
<p>In March 2008, <i><a href="/wiki/GQ" title="GQ">GQ</a></i> magazine ran with an interview conducted by <a href="/wiki/Piers_Morgan" title="Piers Morgan">Piers Morgan</a> in which Clegg admitted to sleeping with "no more than 30" women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> Senior Lib Dem MPs defended his comments; <a href="/wiki/Lembit_%C3%96pik" title="Lembit Öpik">Lembit Öpik</a> said it showed "you can be a human being and a party Leader", and <a href="/wiki/Norman_Lamb" title="Norman Lamb">Norman Lamb</a> that "Nick tries to be absolutely straight in everything that he does, and that might sometimes get him into trouble but he will build a reputation for being honest and straightforward."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> Speaking to the BBC about the interview Clegg said "wisdom with hindsight is an easy thing" as what had been a split second response had been "taken out of context, interpreted, over interpreted and so on".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Relationships_with_the_frontbench">Relationships with the frontbench</span></h3>
<p>Upon his election Clegg appointed leadership rival Huhne as his replacement as Home Affairs spokesperson and following his strong performances as acting party leader, Vince Cable was retained as the main Treasury spokesperson. Media commentators noted that the Clegg-Huhne-Cable triumvirate provided the Liberal Democrats with an effective political team for the coming years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> On 5 March 2008, Clegg suffered a real test following the resignation of three of his front bench team. <a href="/wiki/David_Heath_(politician)" title="David Heath (politician)">David Heath</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alistair_Carmichael" title="Alistair Carmichael">Alistair Carmichael</a> and Tim Farron had been told to abstain in the vote for a referendum on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lisbon_Treaty" title="Lisbon Treaty">Lisbon Treaty</a> but had wanted to vote in favour and so defied the whip. In addition to the three frontbenchers, a further 12 more backbench LibDem MPs also defied the whip and voted "yes". Clegg said "though we have disagreed on this issue I fully understand and respect their strongly held views on the subject.... However, as they have recognised, the shadow cabinet cannot operate effectively unless the principle of collective responsibility is maintained."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p>The resignations happened not long after <a href="/wiki/Michael_Martin,_Baron_Martin_of_Springburn" title="Michael Martin, Baron Martin of Springburn">Michael Martin</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Speaker_of_the_House_of_Commons_(United_Kingdom)" title="Speaker of the House of Commons (United Kingdom)">Speaker of the House of Commons</a>, had on 26 February 2008 blocked calls by the Liberal Democrats for an "in or out" referendum on Britain's EU membership. The Speaker's authority was called into question when, led by Nick Clegg, the Liberal Democrats marched out of the House of Commons, calling the Speaker's decision a constitutional "outrage". Just moments earlier, frontbench foreign affairs spokesman for the party <a href="/wiki/Ed_Davey" title="Ed Davey">Ed Davey</a> had been expelled from the chamber by the Speaker's deputy, Sir <a href="/wiki/Michael_Lord" title="Michael Lord">Michael Lord</a>, for further challenging the ruling.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup>
</p><p>In November 2008, Clegg suffered more allegations of difficulties with the front bench following an article in the <i><a href="/wiki/Daily_Mirror" title="Daily Mirror">Daily Mirror</a></i> that reported that Clegg had criticised senior members of his front bench whilst on a plane journey. He told the BBC's Politics Show that "a lot of it is, frankly, fiction".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:25em; ;"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303512">.mw-parser-output .quotebox{background-color:#F9F9F9;border:1px solid #aaa;box-sizing:border-box;padding:10px;font-size:88%}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft{margin:0.5em 1.4em 0.8em 0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright{margin:0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.centered{margin:0.5em auto 0.8em auto}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft p,.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright p{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-title{background-color:#F9F9F9;text-align:center;font-size:larger;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:before{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" “ ";vertical-align:-45%;line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:after{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" ” ";line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .left-aligned{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .right-aligned{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .center-aligned{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .quotebox cite{display:block;font-style:normal}@media screen and (max-width:360px){.mw-parser-output .quotebox{min-width:100%;margin:0 0 0.8em!important;float:none!important}}</style>
<div class="quotebox-quote quoted left-aligned" style="">"I believe every single person is extraordinary.
<p>The tragedy is that we have a society where too many people never get to fulfil that extraordinary potential.
My view – the liberal view – is that government's job is to help them to do it.
Not to tell people how to live their lives.
But to make their choices possible, to release their potential, no matter who they are.
The way to do that is to take power away from those who hoard it.
To challenge vested interests.
To break down privilege.
To clear out the bottlenecks in our society that block opportunity and block progress.
</p>
And so give everyone a chance to live the life they want."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup></div>
<p><cite class="left-aligned" style="">Liberal Democrat Manifesto Launch, 14 April 2010</cite>
</p>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Attitudes_to_other_parties">Attitudes to other parties</span></h3>
<p>In the Commons, Clegg initially concentrated most of his fire on Labour and Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Gordon_Brown" title="Gordon Brown">Gordon Brown</a>, but in the autumn of 2009 began also focusing on Cameron and the Conservatives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> Clegg rejected an appeal from Cameron for their two parties to work together.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-news.bbc.co.uk_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-news.bbc.co.uk-92">[92]</a></sup> Clegg argued that the Conservatives were totally different from his party, and that the Lib Dems were the true "progressives" in UK politics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-news.bbc.co.uk_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-news.bbc.co.uk-92">[92]</a></sup> At the 2009 party conference in Bournemouth, he accused the Conservatives of "simply believing it is their turn" and claimed that come the election the "choice before people is the choice between fake, phoney change from David Cameron's Conservatives, and real change the Liberal Democrats offer".
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Parliamentary_expenses">Parliamentary expenses</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Clegg_Victory_for_the_Gurkhas.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1066" data-file-width="1600" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Clegg_Victory_for_the_Gurkhas.jpg/220px-Clegg_Victory_for_the_Gurkhas.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Clegg_Victory_for_the_Gurkhas.jpg/330px-Clegg_Victory_for_the_Gurkhas.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Clegg_Victory_for_the_Gurkhas.jpg/440px-Clegg_Victory_for_the_Gurkhas.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Clegg_Victory_for_the_Gurkhas.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Clegg with <a href="/wiki/David_Cameron" title="David Cameron">David Cameron</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Chris_Huhne" title="Chris Huhne">Chris Huhne</a> in 2009</div></div></div>
<p>Clegg became the first party leader in modern political history to call for a Speaker of the House of Commons to resign, describing then-Speaker Michael Martin, following his handling of the expenses scandal, as a defender of the status quo and an obstacle to the reform of Parliament.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-typepad1_37-4"><a href="#cite_note-typepad1-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup>
</p><p>In response to revelations about MPs' expenses, Clegg set out his plans for reform of Parliament in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Guardian" title="The Guardian">The Guardian</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> Speaking about the plans, he said: "let us bar the gates of Westminster and stop MPs leaving for their summer holidays until this crisis has been sorted out, and every nook and cranny of our political system has been reformed." He argued for the "reinvention of British politics" within 100 days, calling for a commitment to accept the Kelly expenses report in full; the power to <a href="/wiki/Recall_election" title="Recall election">recall</a> members suspended for misconduct; <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lords" title="House of Lords">House of Lords</a> reform; reform of party funding; fixed-term parliaments; enabling legislation for a referendum on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alternative_Vote_Top-up" title="Alternative Vote Top-up">AV+</a>; and changes to House of Commons procedure to reduce executive power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-takebackpower1_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-takebackpower1-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>Shortly ahead of the election, Clegg was asked about his own expenses by <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Neil" title="Andrew Neil">Andrew Neil</a> of the BBC. Clegg allegedly claimed the full amount permissible under the Additional Cost Allowance, including claims for food, gardening and redecorating his second home. <i>The Telegraph</i> also said Clegg claimed £80 for international call charges, a claim he said he would repay.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Perspective">Perspective</span></h3>
<p>Clegg has aimed to modernise the Liberal Democrat Party at the same time as maintaining its traditions of political and philosophical Liberalism. In 2011, he told a party conference that the Liberal Democrats were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radical_center_(politics)" title="Radical center (politics)">radical centrist</a> in orientation:
</p>
<blockquote><p>
Our opponents try to divide us with their outdated labels of left and right. But we are not on the left and we are not on the right. We have our own label: Liberal. We are liberals and we own the freehold to the centre ground of British politics. Our politics is the politics of the radical centre.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Policies">Policies</span></h3>
<p>Since becoming leader of the Liberal Democrats, Clegg has called for more choice for patients on waiting lists in the <a href="/wiki/National_Health_Service" title="National Health Service">National Health Service</a> (NHS), giving them the option to go private and to be funded by the NHS if they wish; a substantial tax cut to "put more money back into the pockets of people", better action on the environment, the abandonment of Britain's Trident missile-defence system, <a href="/wiki/Fixed-term_election" title="Fixed-term election">fixed-term parliaments</a>; devolving more power to local councils; giving constituents the power to force a by-election if their MP was found responsible for serious wrongdoing; and a slimming of government across the board.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> Clegg campaigned to cut spending on defence projects such as <a href="/wiki/Eurofighter_Typhoon" title="Eurofighter Typhoon">Eurofighter</a> as well as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UK_Trident_programme" title="UK Trident programme">UK Trident programme</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> As regards public spending, at the party's 2009 conference in Bournemouth Clegg argued for "savage" spending cuts and said politicians need to treat voters "like grown ups" whilst accusing the Labour and Conservative parties of indulging in "childish games" over the "c-word".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gurkha_campaign">Gurkha campaign</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nick_Clegg_being_presented_a_Gurkha_Hat,_by_a_Gurkha_veteran.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="300" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Nick_Clegg_being_presented_a_Gurkha_Hat%2C_by_a_Gurkha_veteran.jpg/220px-Nick_Clegg_being_presented_a_Gurkha_Hat%2C_by_a_Gurkha_veteran.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e3/Nick_Clegg_being_presented_a_Gurkha_Hat%2C_by_a_Gurkha_veteran.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nick_Clegg_being_presented_a_Gurkha_Hat,_by_a_Gurkha_veteran.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Nick Clegg being presented with a Gurkha hat by a Gurkha veteran during his <a href="/wiki/Maidstone" title="Maidstone">Maidstone</a> visit to celebrate the success of their joint campaign for the right to live in Britain, 2009</div></div></div>
<p>On 29 April 2009 the Liberal Democrats proposed in the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="House of Commons of the United Kingdom">House of Commons</a> to offer all Gurkhas an equal right of residence; the motion resulted in a defeat for the Government by 267 votes to 246. It was the only first day motion defeat for a government since 1978. On speaking about the result Clegg said "this is an immense victory [...] for the rights of Gurkhas who have been waiting so long for justice, a victory for Parliament, a victory for decency". He added that it was "the kind of thing people want this country to do".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 21 May 2009, the Home Secretary <a href="/wiki/Jacqui_Smith" title="Jacqui Smith">Jacqui Smith</a> announced that all Gurkha veterans who retired before 1997 with at least four years' service could settle in the UK. The actress and daughter of Gurkha corps Major James Lumley, <a href="/wiki/Joanna_Lumley" title="Joanna Lumley">Joanna Lumley</a>, who had highlighted the treatment of the Gurkhas and campaigned for their rights, commented: "This is the welcome we have always longed to give".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Deputy_Prime_Minister_.282010.E2.80.932015.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Deputy_Prime_Minister_(2010–2015)">Deputy Prime Minister (2010–2015)</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Cameron%E2%80%93Clegg_coalition" title="Cameron–Clegg coalition">Cameron–Clegg coalition</a></div>
<p>Clegg became <a href="/wiki/Deputy_Prime_Minister_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Deputy Prime Minister of the United Kingdom">Deputy Prime Minister of the United Kingdom</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lord_President_of_the_Council" title="Lord President of the Council">Lord President of the Council</a> on 11 May 2010 through a coalition with the Conservative Party under Prime Minister David Cameron.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-politicshome.com_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-politicshome.com-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-uk.reuters.com_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-uk.reuters.com-103">[103]</a></sup> He was also made Minister for Constitutional and Political Reform, which was a key point for the Liberal Democrats during the creation of the coalition.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Coalition_Agreement">The Coalition Agreement</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Conservative%E2%80%93Liberal_Democrat_coalition_agreement" title="Conservative–Liberal Democrat coalition agreement">Conservative–Liberal Democrat coalition agreement</a></div>
<p>The morning after the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_2010" title="United Kingdom general election, 2010">2010 general election</a> presented the country with no one political party able to form a government that would command a majority in the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="House of Commons of the United Kingdom">House of Commons</a>. In light of this reality the Conservative leader, David Cameron, went public and gave a "big, open and comprehensive offer" to the Lib Dem leader and said that he wanted to open up negotiations with the Liberal Democrats to form Britain's first <a href="/wiki/Coalition_government" title="Coalition government">coalition government</a> since the Second World War. In reply, Clegg said that he had always maintained that the party with the most seats and the most votes should have the right to seek to govern. Speaking to the press he said: "It seems this morning that it is the Conservative Party which has more votes and more seats – although not an absolute majority – which is why I now think that it is the Conservative Party which should seek to govern in the national interest."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup>
</p><p>Following the announcement, teams of negotiators from both parties formulated what would become the <a href="/wiki/Conservative%E2%80%93Liberal_Democrat_coalition_agreement" title="Conservative–Liberal Democrat coalition agreement">Coalition Agreement</a> which would form the basis of their partnership together.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Gordon_Brown" title="Gordon Brown">Gordon Brown's</a> resignation on 11 May 2010 meant that Cameron was invited by the Queen to form a government<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> and a coalition with the Liberal Democrats was agreed, with Nick Clegg as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deputy_Prime_Minister" title="Deputy Prime Minister">Deputy Prime Minister</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Plans_for_electoral_reform">Plans for electoral reform</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Parliamentary_Voting_System_and_Constituencies_Bill">Parliamentary Voting System and Constituencies Bill</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Parliamentary_Voting_System_and_Constituencies_Act_2011" title="Parliamentary Voting System and Constituencies Act 2011">Parliamentary Voting System and Constituencies Act 2011</a></div>
<p>On 5 July 2010, Clegg unveiled plans to have fewer MPs and to hold a referendum on the voting system so that the next general election would be contested under the Alternative Vote system. In a statement, he said UK democracy was "fractured", with some votes counting more than others. As part of the statement he also changed initial plans requiring the number of MPs needed to vote to dissolve Parliament from 55% to 66%. The <a href="/wiki/Parliamentary_Voting_System_and_Constituencies_Act_2011" title="Parliamentary Voting System and Constituencies Act 2011">Parliamentary Voting System and Constituencies Bill</a> was presented to parliament on 22 July 2010 for its first reading which if successful would see the date of the referendum on changing the voting system from the current 'first past the post' system to the <a href="/wiki/Instant-runoff_voting" title="Instant-runoff voting">Alternative Vote</a> (AV) system set for 5 May 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbc-109">[109]</a></sup>
</p><p>The bill also introduced plans to reduce the number of MP's in the House of Commons from 650 to 600, something the Labour party attacked as <a href="/wiki/Gerrymandering" title="Gerrymandering">gerrymandering</a>, as to do this there would need to be boundary changes. Clegg told MPs: "Together, these proposals help correct the deep unfairness in the way we hold elections in this country. Under the current set-up, votes count more in some parts of the country than others, and millions feel that their votes don't count at all. Elections are won and lost in a small minority of seats. We have a fractured democracy, where some people's votes count and other people's votes don't count."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc_109-1"><a href="#cite_note-bbc-109">[109]</a></sup> On 22 July 2010, the question for the referendum on AV was published, asking voters if they wish to "adopt the 'alternative vote' system instead of the current 'first past the post' system" for electing MPs". The question required a yes or no answer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> The Act received Royal Assent on 16 February 2011. The result of the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_Alternative_Vote_referendum,_2011" title="United Kingdom Alternative Vote referendum, 2011">referendum</a> was that the alternative vote proposal was defeated by a margin of 2:1.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Fixed-term_Parliaments_Bill">Fixed-term Parliaments Bill</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Fixed-term_Parliaments_Act_2011" title="Fixed-term Parliaments Act 2011">Fixed-term Parliaments Act 2011</a></div>
<p>Clegg also confirmed that the government planned to introduce legislation for five-year fixed-term parliaments, with elections to be held on the first Thursday in May of the fifth year after the previous general election, starting with 7 May 2015. The corresponding bill was presented to parliament on 22 July 2010 and the <a href="/wiki/Fixed-term_Parliaments_Act_2011" title="Fixed-term Parliaments Act 2011">Fixed-term Parliaments Act 2011</a> received Royal Assent on 15 September 2011.
</p>
<h3><span id="Prime_Minister.27s_Questions"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Prime_Minister's_Questions">Prime Minister's Questions</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rutte-Clegg.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2832" data-file-width="4256" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Rutte-Clegg.jpg/220px-Rutte-Clegg.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Rutte-Clegg.jpg/330px-Rutte-Clegg.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Rutte-Clegg.jpg/440px-Rutte-Clegg.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rutte-Clegg.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Nick Clegg with the <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_the_Netherlands" title="Prime Minister of the Netherlands">Prime Minister of the Netherlands</a> <a href="/wiki/Mark_Rutte" title="Mark Rutte">Mark Rutte</a> on 15 November 2010</div></div></div>
<p>On 21 July 2010, Clegg became the first Liberal Democrat leader to answer for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prime_minister%27s_questions" title="Prime minister's questions">Prime minister's questions</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> He courted controversy during the exchange when at the despatch box he attacked shadow justice secretary <a href="/wiki/Jack_Straw" title="Jack Straw">Jack Straw</a> for the decision to invade Iraq, saying "perhaps one day you could account for your role in the most disastrous decision of all, which is the illegal <a href="/wiki/2003_invasion_of_Iraq" title="2003 invasion of Iraq">invasion of Iraq</a>." Despite having long held views about the issue, the comment was controversial, as it did not reflect the policy of the government, which was that the legality of the war in Iraq was currently being studied by the Iraq inquiry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup>
</p><p>Clegg next stepped in for Prime Minister's Questions on 8 September 2010 following the news that Cameron's father had taken very ill. Standing in for the Labour deputy leader <a href="/wiki/Harriet_Harman" title="Harriet Harman">Harriet Harman</a>, Jack Straw challenged Clegg on the allegations of phone hacking against Downing Street's director of communications <a href="/wiki/Andy_Coulson" title="Andy Coulson">Andy Coulson</a>. Responding, Clegg claimed that the allegations dating from Coulson's time at the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/News_of_the_World_phone_hacking_affair" title="News of the World phone hacking affair">News of the World</a></i> were a matter for the police to investigate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> On 10 November 2010, as Cameron was making a trade visit to China, Clegg deputised for the third time, meeting Harman across the despatch box. On a day that coincided with violent student protests against tuition fees in London, the Labour deputy leader chose the same subject to quiz Clegg, accusing him of a U-turn on pledges made before the election. Responding, Clegg accused Harman of trying to re-position the Labour Party as the party of students when the party had previously campaigned against fees only to end up introducing them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tuition_fees">Tuition fees</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tuition_fees_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Tuition fees in the United Kingdom">Tuition fees in the United Kingdom</a></div>
<p>The issue of student financing had been considered one of the flagship policies of the Liberal Democrats with all of the party's MPs, including Nick Clegg, signing the <a href="/wiki/Vote_for_Students_pledge" title="Vote for Students pledge">Vote for Students pledge</a> to oppose any increase in student tuition fees prior to the 2010 general election.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup>  As part of the coalition agreement the Lib Dems abandoned their pledge to oppose any increase in tuition fees but gained permission to abstain on any vote relating to the increase of tuition fees. The <a href="/wiki/Browne_Review" title="Browne Review">Browne Review</a> recommended that the present cap on student fees be lifted, potentially paving the way for universities to charge much higher fees in the future.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup>
</p><p>Clegg wrote to his MPs saying that he had "struggled endlessly" with the issue and said that departing from the pledge he had made prior to the election would be "one of the most difficult decisions of my political career". Defending recommendations of the review, Clegg said that poorer students would pay less since the income level at which students needed to earn before beginning to pay off their student loan would rise from £15,000 to £21,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup>
</p><p>During an interview on 24 October 2010 with the BBC's <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Marr" title="Andrew Marr">Andrew Marr</a>, Clegg said that he "regretted" not being able to keep his pre-election policy to scrap tuition fees but claimed that this was a result of the financial situation the country had found itself in.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 19 September 2012, Clegg apologised, not for breaking his pledge, but for having "made a promise we weren't absolutely sure we could deliver".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> The apology was <a href="/wiki/Nick_Clegg_Says_I%27m_Sorry_(The_Autotune_Remix)" title="Nick Clegg Says I'm Sorry (The Autotune Remix)">parodied in a song</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fairness_premium">Fairness premium</span></h3>
<p>On 14 October 2010, Clegg delivered a speech at a school in <a href="/wiki/Chesterfield" title="Chesterfield">Chesterfield</a>, at which he announced the government's intention to spend £7 billion on a 'fairness premium' designed to see extra support going to the poorest pupils over the course of the parliament. Clegg claimed that the funds for the scheme would be "additional" to the current education budget and this view was backed up by a <a href="/wiki/10_Downing_Street" title="10 Downing Street">Number 10</a> aide who when interviewed by <i>The Guardian</i> said "the money for this will come from outside the education budget. We're not just rearranging furniture – this is real new money from elsewhere in Whitehall."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> The package announced would provide 15 hours a week free nursery education for the poorest two-year-olds and a 'pupil premium' which would be given to schools to help those pupils eligible for free school meals worth £2.5 billion a year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup>
</p><p>The announcement by Clegg ensured that two elements of the government's Coalition Agreement had been fulfilled, that of the promise to support free nursery care to pre-school children and that of funding a 'significant premium for disadvantaged pupils from outside the schools budget by reductions in spending elsewhere'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> For Clegg the announcement was an important one politically coming two days after the publication of the Browne Review into the future of university funding which signalled the reversal of the long cherished Liberal Democrat policy of opposing any increase in tuition fees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> The pupil premium announcement was important as it formed one of the four key 'priorities' on which the party had fought the last election.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-libdems6_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-libdems6-8">[8]</a></sup> On 20 October 2010, the plans for the 'fairness premium' were introduced by the Treasury as part of the spending review which said that the money would be introduced over the period of the review which "will support the poorest in the early years and at every stage of their education".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bank_shares">Bank shares</span></h3>
<p>In June 2011, Clegg proposed that more than 46 million people would be handed shares in Royal Bank of Scotland and Lloyds Banking Group under the "people's bank" plan. The plan proposes that ordinary voters would be able to profit from any increase in the value of their shares once the Treasury has recouped taxpayers' money used for the bail-out – an offer that could eventually be worth up to £1,000 to householders. Clegg said that it was "psychologically immensely important" for people to be given a stake in the banks in the wake of the financial crisis. "Their money has been used to the tune of billions and billions and billions to keep the British banking system on a life-support system," he said. The taxpayer owns 84 per cent of RBS and 43 per cent of Lloyds after the Government spent £65.8 billion buying shares at the height of the financial crisis. The share price of both banks has fallen sharply since the bail-out.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup>
</p><p>Aides close to Cameron and <a href="/wiki/George_Osborne" title="George Osborne">George Osborne</a> warned that the Liberal Democrat scheme could cost £250 million to establish and would prove an "administrative nightmare". However <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Williams_(politician)" title="Stephen Williams (politician)">Stephen Williams</a> said "We are absolutely convinced it (standard privatisation) would not be cheaper, we are absolutely convinced of that."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> A Downing Street spokesman said that the Liberal Democrat plan was "an option". "The Treasury has said it is going to look at all the options and this will be one of those options," the spokesman said. "We will be driven by making sure that we deliver the best value for the taxpayer." The Treasury also played down the likelihood of the proposal becoming reality. A source said Mr Osborne was "happy to listen to ideas" but the "issue doesn't currently arise".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="House_of_Lords_reform">House of Lords reform</span></h3>
<p>In August 2012, after <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lords_Reform_Bill_2012" title="House of Lords Reform Bill 2012">reform of the House of Lords</a> was abandoned, Clegg said the Conservatives had defied the Coalition agreement by trying to "pick and choose" which items of Government policy they support. The row marked one of the most serious crises for the Coalition since the 2010 general election. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jeremy_Hunt_(politician)" title="Jeremy Hunt (politician)">Jeremy Hunt</a>, the Culture Secretary, said he was "very disappointed", describing the decision as a "great shame". Clegg said that favoured by the Conservatives to make sure the Coalition is a fair and equal partnership. "My party has held to that [Coalition] contract even when it meant voting for things that we found difficult," he said. "But the Conservative party is not honouring the commitment to Lords reform and, as a result, part of our contract has now been broken." Clegg also revealed the Conservatives rejected his suggestion of a "last ditch" compromise to save both policies. "Clearly I cannot permit a situation where Conservative rebels can pick and choose the parts of the contract they like, while Liberal Democrat MPs are bound to the entire agreement," he said.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup>
</p><p>In September 2012, Clegg formally announced that he was "regrettably" withdrawing proposals to reform the Lords in the face of overwhelming opposition from Conservative MPs. He signalled he would exact his revenge by refusing to sack any Liberal Democrat minister who voted against changes to MPs' boundaries – which is Government policy – in retaliation over the Lords reform débâcle. Traditionally party leaders are offered peerages when they leave the House of Commons. When asked by Labour MP <a href="/wiki/Dennis_Skinner" title="Dennis Skinner">Dennis Skinner</a> if he would take a seat in the Lords, he said: "No", adding: "I personally will not take a seat in an unreformed House of Lords. It just sticks in the throat."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Opposition_.282015.E2.80.932017.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Opposition_(2015–2017)">Opposition (2015–2017)</span></h2>
<p>Clegg retained his Sheffield Hallam seat in the 2015 election, despite a strong campaign from Labour with a 17% swing in the vote.
