Clegg campaigned for Remain in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2016_EU_referendum" title="2016 EU referendum">2016 EU referendum</a>, as did most other Liberal Democrats, and became the Lib Dem spokesperson for <a href="/wiki/Department_for_Exiting_the_European_Union" title="Department for Exiting the European Union">Exiting the European Union</a> and <a href="/wiki/Department_for_International_Trade" title="Department for International Trade">International Trade</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Electoral_performance_and_standing_in_the_polls">Electoral performance and standing in the polls</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Standing_in_the_polls">Standing in the polls</span></h3>
<p>After Clegg became leader, the polls were mixed; the Liberal Democrats occasionally polled above 20 points,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> averaging around 19%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> In May 2009, the party overtook Labour in an opinion poll (25%–22%) for the first time since the days of its predecessor, the <a href="/wiki/SDP%E2%80%93Liberal_Alliance" title="SDP–Liberal Alliance">SDP–Liberal Alliance</a>, in 1987.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> Clegg thus became the first Liberal Democrat leader to out-poll Labour in an opinion poll. After Clegg's performance in the first of three <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election_debates,_2010" title="United Kingdom general election debates, 2010">general election debates</a> on 15 April 2010, there was an unprecedented surge of media attention and support for the Liberal Democrats in opinion polls. <a href="/wiki/ComRes" title="ComRes">ComRes</a> reported the Liberal Democrats at 24% on the day,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> and on 20 April in a <a href="/wiki/YouGov" title="YouGov">YouGov</a> poll, the Liberal Democrats were on 34%, one point above the Conservatives, with Labour in third place on 28%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> This success was described as <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Cleggmania" title="wikt:Cleggmania">"Cleggmania"</a> by journalists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup>
</p><p>Following the formation of the coalition, support for the Liberal Democrats fell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thisislondon1_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-thisislondon1-9">[9]</a></sup> On 8 December 2010, the eve of a House of Commons vote on changes in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tuition_fees_(UK)" title="Tuition fees (UK)">funding of higher education</a>, an opinion poll conducted by <a href="/wiki/YouGov" title="YouGov">YouGov</a> recorded voting intention figures of Conservatives 41%, <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">Labour</a> 41%, <a href="/wiki/List_of_political_parties_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="List of political parties in the United Kingdom">other parties</a> 11% and Liberal Democrats 8%,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> the lowest level of support recorded for the Liberal Democrats in any opinion poll since September 1990.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Parliamentary_by-elections_.282008.E2.80.932010.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Parliamentary_by-elections_(2008–2010)">Parliamentary by-elections (2008–2010)</span></h3>
<p>Five parliamentary by-elections were held during Clegg's leadership prior to the 2010 general election. At <a href="/wiki/Crewe_and_Nantwich" title="Crewe and Nantwich">Crewe and Nantwich</a> the party's share of the vote decreased by 4%. In the subsequent <a href="/wiki/Henley_by-election,_2008" title="Henley by-election, 2008">Henley by-election</a> the party achieved a 1.8% increase in their vote. At the <a href="/wiki/Norwich_North_by-election,_2009" title="Norwich North by-election, 2009">Norwich North by-election</a> the party came third with a 2.2% fall in their vote share. The two Scottish by-elections, <a href="/wiki/Glenrothes_by-election,_2008" title="Glenrothes by-election, 2008">Glenrothes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Glasgow_East_by-election,_2008" title="Glasgow East by-election, 2008">Glasgow East</a>, saw decreases in the Liberal Democrat vote, 8% and 10% respectively.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2008_and_2009_local_elections">2008 and 2009 local elections</span></h3>
<p>The local election results for the Liberal Democrats during the same period were mixed. In the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_local_elections,_2008" title="United Kingdom local elections, 2008">2008 local elections</a> the Liberal Democrats took second place with 25% of the vote making a net gain of 34 councillors and took control of <a href="/wiki/Sheffield_City_Council" title="Sheffield City Council">Sheffield City Council</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> but their share of the vote was down 1%. <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_local_elections,_2009" title="United Kingdom local elections, 2009">The next year</a> the Liberal Democrats gained <a href="/wiki/Bristol" title="Bristol">Bristol</a> but lost both <a href="/wiki/Somerset" title="Somerset">Somerset</a> and <a href="/wiki/Devon" title="Devon">Devon</a> producing a net loss of councils and a net loss of one councillor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> The party however did increase its share of the vote by 3% to 28% beating the Labour Party into third place. In the <a href="/wiki/European_Parliament_election,_2009_(United_Kingdom)" title="European Parliament election, 2009 (United Kingdom)">European Parliament elections</a> held on the same day, the Liberal Democrats gained a seat but had a slight decrease in their share of the vote, staying in 4th place compared to the previous European elections, behind the two main parties and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_Independence_Party" title="United Kingdom Independence Party">UKIP</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2008_London_elections">2008 London elections</span></h3>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/London_Assembly_election,_2008" title="London Assembly election, 2008">2008 London Assembly elections</a> the Liberal Democrats were the only one of the three main parties to see a decrease in their share of the vote, and in the <a href="/wiki/London_mayoral_election,_2008" title="London mayoral election, 2008">mayoral election</a> the Liberal Democrat candidate <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brian_Paddick" title="Brian Paddick">Brian Paddick</a> came third again with a decreased share of the vote.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2010_general_election">2010 general election</span></h3>
<p>At the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_2010" title="United Kingdom general election, 2010">2010 general election</a>, the Liberal Democrats won 23% of the vote, an improvement of 1%, however they only won 57 seats, 5 fewer than in 2005. No political party had an overall majority, resulting in the nation's first <a href="/wiki/Hung_parliament" title="Hung parliament">hung parliament</a> since <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_February_1974" title="United Kingdom general election, February 1974">February 1974</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> Talks between Cameron, the Conservative Party leader, and Clegg led to an agreed Conservative/Liberal Democrat coalition, enabling <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_II" title="Elizabeth II">the Queen</a> to invite Cameron to form a government.
</p>
<h3><span id="Parliamentary_by-elections_.282010_onwards.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Parliamentary_by-elections_(2010_onwards)">Parliamentary by-elections (2010 onwards)</span></h3>
<p>Since the 2010 general election, Clegg's Liberal Democrats have contested 13 by-elections in Great Britain (as of 2 March 2013).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> The party scored their first by-election win of Clegg's leadership at <a href="/wiki/Eastleigh_by-election,_2013" title="Eastleigh by-election, 2013">Eastleigh</a> in 2013, with <a href="/wiki/Mike_Thornton_(politician)" title="Mike Thornton (politician)">Mike Thornton</a> holding the seat for the Liberal Democrats, despite a 19% swing away from the party. Clegg described the result as an election in which Liberal Democrats "overcame the odds with a stunning victory."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup>
</p><p>Earlier by-elections in the parliament had proven less successful. They failed to win <a href="/wiki/Oldham_East_and_Saddleworth_by-election,_2011" title="Oldham East and Saddleworth by-election, 2011">Oldham East and Saddleworth</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup> in January 2011, after they had successfully petitioned to overturn the general election result. They polled 32% of the vote, a small increase on 2010, but lost out to Labour whose vote was up by 10 percentage points. The Liberal Democrats also came second at <a href="/wiki/Leicester_South_by-election,_2011" title="Leicester South by-election, 2011">Leicester South</a> (which they had held between <a href="/wiki/Leicester_South_by-election,_2004" title="Leicester South by-election, 2004">2004</a> and <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_2005" title="United Kingdom general election, 2005">2005</a>) in May 2011 with 23% (down 4% on 2010),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> and at <a href="/wiki/Manchester_Central_by-election,_2012" title="Manchester Central by-election, 2012">Manchester Central</a> in November 2012 where they polled 9% (down 17%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the remaining nine contests, Liberal Democrats have finished no higher than third place (and in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rotherham_by-election,_2013" title="Rotherham by-election, 2013">Rotherham</a> finished in an unprecedented 8th position, with just 451 votes, or 2% of the total).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup> In every by-election except Oldham East and Saddleworth their vote has fallen, with decreases of over 10% recorded at eight of the contests. In six of the 13 by-elections, the party have lost their deposit after failing to poll 5% of the vote – an unusually high number of such lost deposits for a major party.
</p>
<h3><span id="2011_local.2C_Scottish_and_Welsh_elections"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="2011_local,_Scottish_and_Welsh_elections">2011 local, Scottish and Welsh elections</span></h3>
<p>A year following the formation of the Coalition Clegg's Liberal Democrats faced poor results in the local elections. In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scottish_Parliament_general_election,_2011" title="Scottish Parliament general election, 2011">Scotland</a> the party lost all its mainland constituency seats, holding only the <a href="/wiki/Shetland_(Scottish_Parliament_constituency)" title="Shetland (Scottish Parliament constituency)">Shetland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Orkney_(Scottish_Parliament_constituency)" title="Orkney (Scottish Parliament constituency)">Orkney</a> islands. Their constituency vote share also fell from 16% to just 8%<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/National_Assembly_for_Wales_election,_2011" title="National Assembly for Wales election, 2011">Welsh elections</a> the party held just one of its 3 constituency seats, that of Welsh leader <a href="/wiki/Kirsty_Williams" title="Kirsty Williams">Kirsty Williams</a>, but gained <a href="/wiki/South_Wales_Central_(National_Assembly_for_Wales_electoral_region)" title="South Wales Central (National Assembly for Wales electoral region)">a regional seat</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_local_elections,_2011" title="United Kingdom local elections, 2011">2011 local elections</a>, the Lib Dems lost over 700 councillors, and slumped from 25% to 17% in the share of the local council vote, also losing control of Sheffield City Council with the LibDems dropping to the lowest number of councillors in more than 20 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_Alternative_Vote_referendum,_2011" title="United Kingdom Alternative Vote referendum, 2011">AV referendum</a>, the Yes vote, supported by the Liberal Democrats, was defeated by 67.9% to 32.1%. In the face of the election results, Clegg told the BBC that Liberal Democrats must "get up, dust ourselves down and move on".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2012_local_and_London_elections">2012 local and London elections</span></h3>
<p>Local elections were held in May 2012 to 185 local authorities in Great Britain, including all 32 councils in Scotland and 21 out of 22 in Wales.
</p><p>Results again proved poor for the Liberal Democrats, as they won 431 seats in total, a loss of over 300 on the pre-election position.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup> They also lost overall control of one council (<a href="/wiki/Cambridge_City_Council" title="Cambridge City Council">Cambridge</a>, though the Liberal Democrats hold 21 out of 42 seats, so they exercise control with the mayor's casting vote<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup>). They retained control of the other six councils they were defending in England. Despite the losses, the Liberal Democrat vote share saw a modest increase compared to 2011.
</p><p>Elections were also held for the Mayoralties of <a href="/wiki/City_of_Salford" title="City of Salford">Salford</a> and <a href="/wiki/Liverpool_City_Council" title="Liverpool City Council">Liverpool</a>. Liberal Democrat candidates polled 5% and 6% respectively, with Labour winning both contests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup>
</p><p>In London, elections were held to the <a href="/wiki/London_Assembly_election,_2012" title="London Assembly election, 2012">London Assembly</a> and <a href="/wiki/London_mayoral_election,_2012" title="London mayoral election, 2012">Mayoralty</a>. The Liberal Democrats again selected <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brian_Paddick" title="Brian Paddick">Brian Paddick</a> as their Mayoral candidate. He polled just 4% of the vote (down from 10% in <a href="/wiki/London_mayoral_election,_2008" title="London mayoral election, 2008">2008</a>), and finished fourth behind the <a href="/wiki/Green_Party_of_England_and_Wales" title="Green Party of England and Wales">Green Party</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup> In the Assembly, the Liberal Democrats also finished behind the Greens across London, and failed to win any of the individual constituency seats. They polled 7% of the vote on the London-wide list (which elects "top-up" candidates to the assembly under a form of proportional representation), which represented a decline of 5% on the previous contest. This meant that the party lost one seat, and was reduced to just two assembly seats, their smallest representation since the formation of the assembly in 2000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the aftermath of the results, Clegg again faced calls to quit as leader,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup> with former MP <a href="/wiki/Lembit_%C3%96pik" title="Lembit Öpik">Lembit Öpik</a> suggesting that Clegg retain his Cabinet position while relinquishing leadership of the party, saying "My empirical view is that we would have done better with a different leader".
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2012_Police_and_Crime_Commissioner_elections">2012 Police and Crime Commissioner elections</span></h3>
<p>As part of the Coalition Agreement, directly elected <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Police_and_Crime_Commissioners" title="Police and Crime Commissioners">Police and Crime Commissioners</a> were introduced to replace <a href="/wiki/Police_authority" title="Police authority">Police Authorities</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/England_and_Wales_police_and_crime_commissioner_elections,_2012" title="England and Wales police and crime commissioner elections, 2012">Elections</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup> to the new posts took place in November 2012. Liberal Democrats contested 24 of the 41 police force areas, and failed to win any of the contests (and in fact never progressed to the second round of the two-stage count in any of the elections they fought). Their best performance was in <a href="/wiki/Cumbria_Constabulary" title="Cumbria Constabulary">Cumbria</a>, where they polled 22%, while their worst was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Surrey_Constabulary" title="Surrey Constabulary">Surrey</a> where the took just 6% of the vote.
</p><p>Despite not winning any contests under their official party label, one Liberal Democrat, <a href="/wiki/Winston_Roddick" title="Winston Roddick">Winston Roddick</a> was elected as Police and Crime Commissioner for <a href="/wiki/North_Wales_Police" title="North Wales Police">North Wales</a> having stood as an Independent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup> Roddick claimed that he had never hidden his party membership and that his campaign was "financed by himself with no donations or backing from any political party and he was an independent candidate in every sense of the word". His campaign also dismissed as "sour grapes" claims from the Labour Party that "the only way in which the Lib Dems thought they could win the election was by presenting themselves as independent."
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2015_and_2017_general_elections">2015 and 2017 general elections</span></h3>
<p>In the 2015 general election, the Liberal Democrats were reduced from 57 seats to 8. Clegg held his Sheffield Hallam seat with a reduced majority. After his party's result, he resigned the party leadership the day after the election.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 2017 general election, Clegg lost to Labour candidate <a href="/wiki/Jared_O%27Mara" title="Jared O'Mara">Jared O'Mara</a> by a margin of 2,125 votes (4.2%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-telegraph2_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-telegraph2-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>Clegg was appointed a <a href="/wiki/Knight_Bachelor" title="Knight Bachelor">Knight Bachelor</a> in the <a href="/wiki/2018_New_Year_Honours" title="2018 New Year Honours">2018 New Year Honours</a> for political and public service.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Brexit">Brexit</span></h2>
<p>In May 2018 Clegg joined <a href="/wiki/David_Miliband" title="David Miliband">David Miliband</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nicky_Morgan" title="Nicky Morgan">Nicky Morgan</a> calling for a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soft_Brexit" title="Soft Brexit">soft Brexit</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup> On 23 June 2018 Clegg appeared at the <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Vote" title="People's Vote">People's Vote</a> march in London to mark the second anniversary of <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_European_Union_membership_referendum,_2016" title="United Kingdom European Union membership referendum, 2016">the referendum to leave the European Union</a>. People's Vote is a campaign group calling for a public vote on the final Brexit deal between the UK and the European Union.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Broadcasting_and_media">Broadcasting and media</span></h2>
<p>Since January 2013 Clegg has presented a weekly radio show on <a href="/wiki/LBC" title="LBC">LBC</a> called <i>Call Clegg</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup> Initially broadcast in the London area, the programme went national along with LBC in February 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup> The programme was nominated for two <a href="/wiki/Radio_Academy_Awards" title="Radio Academy Awards">Radio Academy Awards</a> in 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup> Since April 2018, Clegg has fronted a podcast called <i>Anger Management with Nick Clegg</i> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup><i>,</i> which he interviews known persons about the politics of anger.  Since the first episode, it has been featured in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Guardian" title="The Guardian">The Guardian</a></i> under Podcast of the Week.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup>
</p><p>A party political broadcast in which Clegg apologised for the Liberal Democrats breaking the promise over tuition fees was remixed into a song, "<a href="/wiki/Nick_Clegg_Says_I%27m_Sorry_(The_Autotune_Remix)" title="Nick Clegg Says I'm Sorry (The Autotune Remix)">Nick Clegg Says I'm Sorry</a>" by <a href="/wiki/The_Poke" title="The Poke">The Poke</a> and Alex Ross, and sold on iTunes as a charity single. The song charted on 23 September 2012 at number 143 in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UK_Singles_Charts" title="UK Singles Charts">Official UK Singles Charts</a> before climbing to 104 the following week.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup> In his 2010 production Dandelion Mind, comedian <a href="/wiki/Bill_Bailey" title="Bill Bailey">Bill Bailey</a> sang "Nick Clegg you don't have to wear that dress tonight, walk the streets for money, you don't have to sell your body to the right" to the tune of "<a href="/wiki/Roxanne_(song)" title="Roxanne (song)">Roxanne</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_life">Personal life</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Clegg_and_Gonz%C3%A1lez_Dur%C3%A1ntez.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1067" data-file-width="1600" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Clegg_and_Gonz%C3%A1lez_Dur%C3%A1ntez.jpg/220px-Clegg_and_Gonz%C3%A1lez_Dur%C3%A1ntez.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Clegg_and_Gonz%C3%A1lez_Dur%C3%A1ntez.jpg/330px-Clegg_and_Gonz%C3%A1lez_Dur%C3%A1ntez.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Clegg_and_Gonz%C3%A1lez_Dur%C3%A1ntez.jpg/440px-Clegg_and_Gonz%C3%A1lez_Dur%C3%A1ntez.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Clegg_and_Gonz%C3%A1lez_Dur%C3%A1ntez.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Clegg with his wife Miriam holding their son Miguel on 23 February 2009</div></div></div>
<p>In September 2000, Clegg married <a href="/wiki/Miriam_Gonz%C3%A1lez_Dur%C3%A1ntez" title="Miriam González Durántez">Miriam González Durántez</a>, from <a href="/wiki/Valladolid" title="Valladolid">Valladolid</a>, Spain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup> They have three sons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup> While Clegg has stated that he does not believe in God,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-typepad1_37-5"><a href="#cite_note-typepad1-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup> his wife is a Roman Catholic and they are bringing up their children as Catholics. On 16 September 2010, during <a href="/wiki/Pope_Benedict_XVI%27s_visit_to_the_United_Kingdom" title="Pope Benedict XVI's visit to the United Kingdom">Pope Benedict XVI's visit to the United Kingdom</a>, Clegg attended the State reception in the grounds of <a href="/wiki/Holyrood_Palace" title="Holyrood Palace">Holyrood Palace</a> and was introduced to the Pope by <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_II" title="Elizabeth II">Her Majesty the Queen</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup> Clegg identifies as a feminist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup>
</p><p>Clegg lives in Parkfields, <a href="/wiki/Putney" title="Putney">Putney</a>, south west London.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup> He also has a house in his former constituency close to the <a href="/wiki/Peak_District" title="Peak District">Peak District</a>, and often walks with his wife near <a href="/wiki/Stanage_Edge" title="Stanage Edge">Stanage Edge</a>, which he describes as "one of the most romantic places in the world".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[182]</a></sup>  In May 2010 Downing Street announced that Clegg and the <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_Foreign_and_Commonwealth_Affairs" title="Secretary of State for Foreign and Commonwealth Affairs">Foreign Secretary</a> <a href="/wiki/William_Hague" title="William Hague">William Hague</a> would share use of <a href="/wiki/Chevening" title="Chevening">Chevening</a>, which is typically the official country residence of the Foreign Secretary of the United Kingdom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup>
</p><p>When he appeared on <i><a href="/wiki/Desert_Island_Discs" title="Desert Island Discs">Desert Island Discs</a></i> in October 2010, his choice of discs included <a href="/wiki/Johnny_Cash" title="Johnny Cash">Johnny Cash</a>, <a href="/wiki/Prince_(musician)" title="Prince (musician)">Prince</a> and <a href="/wiki/Radiohead" title="Radiohead">Radiohead</a> and his luxury was a "stash of cigarettes".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DIDiscs_JH_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-DIDiscs_JH-184">[184]</a></sup> In an interview in April 2011, Clegg stated he dealt with the pressures of political office by reading novels late at night and he "cries regularly to music".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rosa_prince_clegg_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-rosa_prince_clegg-185">[185]</a></sup> He supports <a href="/wiki/Arsenal_F.C." title="Arsenal F.C.">Arsenal F.C.</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Styles_of_address">Styles of address</span></h2>
<ul><li>1967–1999: Mr Nicholas William Peter Clegg</li>
<li>1999–2004: Mr Nicholas William Peter Clegg MEP</li>
<li>2004–2005: Mr Nicholas William Peter Clegg</li>
<li>2005–2008: Mr Nicholas William Peter Clegg MP</li>
<li>2008–2017: The Rt Hon. Nicholas William Peter Clegg MP</li>
<li>2017–2018: The Rt Hon. Nicholas William Peter Clegg</li>
<li>2018–present: The Rt Hon. Sir Nicholas William Peter Clegg</li></ul>

<li id="cite_note-transcript-81"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-transcript_81-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-transcript_81-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20100506022406/http://www.saga.co.uk/media-centre/press-releases/2010/nick-clegg-transcript.asp" rel="nofollow">"Nick Clegg Transcript"</a>. Saga. 25 April 2010. Archived from <a class="external text" href="http://www.saga.co.uk/media-centre/press-releases/2010/nick-clegg-transcript.asp" rel="nofollow">the original</a> on 6 May 2010<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">1 June</span> 2015</span>. <q>...I have always been very open. I was asked a question once in one of those questions where you're only allowed to answer "yes" or "no", and I was asked "Do you believe in God?" As it happens I don't know whether God exists. I'm much more of an agnostic. But of course when I was asked "yes" or "no", I tried (as I always do) to give a straight answer, and I said "Well, no, I don't know". As it happens I have an immense amount of respect for people of faith. I'm not a man of faith ... [but] my wife is, you know, religious. My children are being brought up  my three children are being brought up in her faith. I accompany them to church pretty well every week because, you know, I respect that. Large numbers of my family are, it's just that I am not a man of faith. I know it's fashionable in politics, just as it is fashionable in politics to say that you support a football team even if you haven't since the year of sort of – since you were two years old. It's also fashionable to suddenly pretend that people have faith when they don't. I have always been very open about the fact that I am not a man of faith.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Nick+Clegg+Transcript&amp;rft.pub=Saga&amp;rft.date=2010-04-25&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.saga.co.uk%2Fmedia-centre%2Fpress-releases%2F2010%2Fnick-clegg-transcript.asp&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ANick+Clegg"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></span>
</li>
